    "Rise up, O Judge of the earth (שֹׁפֵט הָאָרֶץ); repay to the proud what they deserve!" The psalmist invokes the same title Abraham used, appealing to the divine Judge for vindication against the wicked. God's role as שֹׁפֵט כָּל־הָאָרֶץ becomes a basis for prayer throughout Scripture.

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
[Genesis 18:32] — THE BARGAINING ENDS: TEN RIGHTEOUS AND THE POWER OF A REMNANT
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────

וַיֹּ֗אמֶר אַל־נָ֤א יִ֙חַר֙ לַֽאדֹנָ֔י וַאֲדַבְּרָ֖ה אַךְ־הַפָּ֑עַם אוּלַ֛י יִמָּצְא֥וּן שָׁ֖ם עֲשָׂרָֽה וַיֹּ֥אמֶר לֹ֥א אַשְׁחִ֖ית בַּעֲב֥וּר הָעֲשָׂרָֽה׃

"He said, 'Oh let not the Lord be angry (אַל־נָא יִחַר לַאדֹנָי), and I will speak just once more (אַךְ־הַפָּעַם). Perhaps ten may be found there.' He said, 'I will not destroy it (לֹא אַשְׁחִית) for the sake of the ten (בַּעֲבוּר הָעֲשָׂרָה).'"

EXEGESIS:
Abraham's intercession reaches its conclusion at ten righteous persons. The number ten (עֲשָׂרָה) later becomes significant in Jewish tradition as the minimum for a מִנְיָן (minyan, quorum for public worship)—perhaps rooted in this passage. Abraham stops at ten, not because God refused further bargaining, but because ten represents a minimal community; fewer than ten would be merely isolated individuals. The tragically implicit answer emerges: not even ten righteous could be found in Sodom. Yet the principle is established: a righteous remnant, however small, can deliver a larger community. The phrase אַל־נָא יִחַר לַאדֹנָי ("let not the Lord be angry") shows Abraham's awareness that persistent intercession risks presumption—yet he continues boldly. The verb אַשְׁחִית ("I will not destroy") uses the hiphil of שׁחת, the same root used for Sodom's moral corruption (שָׁחֵתָה, 18:28)—destruction mirrors the corruption that provoked it. Abraham models intercessory persistence that pushes to the limit of divine patience while never abandoning reverence.

CROSS-REFERENCES:

  → Jeremiah 5:1
    "Run to and fro through the streets of Jerusalem, look and take note! Search her squares to see if you can find a man (אִישׁ), one who does justice (עֹשֶׂה מִשְׁפָּט) and seeks truth, that I may pardon her (וְאֶסְלַח לָהּ)." God offers the same principle to Jerusalem—one righteous person could save the city. The standard for Jerusalem is even lower than Sodom's ten; yet even one could not be found.

  → Isaiah 1:9
    "Unless YHWH of hosts had left us a few survivors (שָׂרִיד כִּמְעָט), we should have been like Sodom (כִּסְדֹם הָיִינוּ), and become like Gomorrah." Isaiah draws the explicit parallel: Jerusalem was preserved only because of a remnant. The Sodom pattern—destruction for lack of righteous—is the negative example Israel barely escaped.

  → Matthew 18:19-20
    "Again I say to you, if two of you agree on earth about anything they ask, it will be done for them by my Father in heaven. For where two or three are gathered in my name, there am I among them." Jesus establishes an even smaller number for effective prayer—the faithful few have disproportionate spiritual influence.

  → 1 Timothy 2:1-4
    "First of all, then, I urge that supplications, prayers, intercessions (ἐντεύξεις), and thanksgivings be made for all people... This is good and pleasing in the sight of God our Savior, who desires all people to be saved." Abraham's intercessory model—pleading for the wicked city—becomes normative for Christian prayer. Believers are called to intercede even for those deserving judgment.

  → Ezekiel 22:30
    "And I sought for a man among them who should build up the wall and stand in the breach (עֹמֵד בַּפֶּרֶץ) before me for the land, that I should not destroy it, but I found none." God sought an intercessor like Abraham for Jerusalem—but found none. The absence of an intercessor sealed Jerusalem's judgment, as the absence of ten righteous sealed Sodom's.

═══════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════
              פרק יט — CHAPTER 19: THE DESTRUCTION OF SODOM AND GOMORRAH
                גָּפְרִית וָאֵשׁ — SULFUR AND FIRE FROM HEAVEN
═══════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
[Genesis 19:1-3] — LOT AT THE GATE: HOSPITALITY AMIDST CORRUPTION
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────

וַיָּבֹ֜אוּ שְׁנֵ֤י הַמַּלְאָכִים֙ סְדֹ֙מָה֙ בָּעֶ֔רֶב וְל֖וֹט יֹשֵׁ֣ב בְּשַֽׁעַר־סְדֹ֑ם וַיַּרְא־לוֹט֙ וַיָּ֣קָם לִקְרָאתָ֔ם וַיִּשְׁתַּ֥חוּ אַפַּ֖יִם אָֽרְצָה׃
וַיֹּ֜אמֶר הִנֶּ֣ה נָּֽא־אֲדֹנַ֗י ס֣וּרוּ נָ֠א אֶל־בֵּ֨ית עַבְדְּכֶ֤ם וְלִ֙ינוּ֙ וְרַחֲצ֣וּ רַגְלֵיכֶ֔ם

"The two angels (שְׁנֵי הַמַּלְאָכִים) came to Sodom in the evening, and Lot was sitting in the gate of Sodom (וְלוֹט יֹשֵׁב בְּשַׁעַר־סְדֹם). When Lot saw them, he rose to meet them and bowed himself with his face to the earth (וַיִּשְׁתַּחוּ אַפַּיִם אָרְצָה). And he said, 'My lords (אֲדֹנַי), please turn aside to your servant's house and spend the night and wash your feet.'"

EXEGESIS:
The narrative notes that שְׁנֵי הַמַּלְאָכִים ("two angels") arrive—confirming that of the three visitors at Mamre, one was YHWH Himself who remained with Abraham while two proceeded to Sodom. Lot's position בְּשַׁעַר־סְדֹם ("in the gate of Sodom") indicates civic prominence—the gate was the place of legal proceedings and community leadership. Lot had risen in Sodom's society, yet this position represents spiritual compromise—he dwells at the decision-making center of a wicked city. His hospitality mirrors Abraham's (bowing, foot-washing, meal preparation), suggesting retained piety amid corrupted surroundings. However, unlike Abraham who entertained freely in the open, Lot's urgent insistence that the visitors not "spend the night in the square" (19:2) reveals awareness of Sodom's danger. He knows what happens to strangers in this city. The contrast with Abraham's serene hospitality highlights the darkness Lot has chosen to inhabit.

CROSS-REFERENCES:

  → Hebrews 13:2
    "Do not neglect to show hospitality to strangers, for thereby some have entertained angels unawares (ἔλαθόν τινες ξενίσαντες ἀγγέλους)." Both Abraham and Lot demonstrate hospitality and receive angelic visitors. The author exhorts believers to continue this tradition—divine messengers may come in human form.

  → 2 Peter 2:7-8
    "And if he rescued righteous Lot (δίκαιον Λώτ), greatly distressed by the sensual conduct of the wicked (for as that righteous man lived among them day after day, he was tormenting his righteous soul over their lawless deeds that he saw and heard)." Peter designates Lot as δίκαιος ("righteous") three times. Despite his compromised position, Lot retained an inner revulsion at Sodom's sin. He did not participate in their wickedness even while dwelling among them.

  → Genesis 13:10-13
    "And Lot lifted up his eyes and saw that the Jordan Valley was well watered everywhere... So Lot chose for himself all the Jordan Valley, and Lot journeyed east... Lot settled among the cities of the valley and moved his tent as far as Sodom. Now the men of Sodom were wicked, great sinners against YHWH." Lot's choice of location reveals the trajectory from attraction (seeing) to proximity (tent near Sodom) to full integration (sitting in Sodom's gate).

  → Psalm 1:1
    "Blessed is the man who walks not in the counsel of the wicked (עֲצַת רְשָׁעִים), nor stands in the way of sinners, nor sits in the seat of scoffers (מוֹשַׁב לֵצִים)." Lot's progression—from looking to pitching his tent to sitting in the gate—mirrors the three stages of spiritual decline: walking, standing, sitting. He illustrates the psalm's negative example.

═══════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════
[Genesis 19:24-26] — SULFUR AND FIRE: THE JUDGMENT AND LOT'S WIFE
═══════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════

וַֽיהוָ֗ה הִמְטִ֧יר עַל־סְדֹ֛ם וְעַל־עֲמֹרָ֖ה גָּפְרִ֣ית וָאֵ֑שׁ מֵאֵ֥ת יְהוָ֖ה מִן־הַשָּׁמָֽיִם׃
וַיַּהֲפֹ֖ךְ אֶת־הֶעָרִ֣ים הָאֵ֑ל וְאֵת֙ כָּל־הַכִּכָּ֔ר וְאֵת֙ כָּל־יֹשְׁבֵ֣י הֶעָרִ֔ים וְצֶ֖מַח הָאֲדָמָֽה׃
וַתַּבֵּ֥ט אִשְׁתּ֖וֹ מֵאַחֲרָ֑יו וַתְּהִ֖י נְצִ֥יב מֶֽלַח׃

"Then YHWH rained on Sodom and Gomorrah sulfur and fire (גָּפְרִית וָאֵשׁ) from YHWH out of heaven (מֵאֵת יְהוָה מִן־הַשָּׁמָיִם). And He overthrew (וַיַּהֲפֹךְ) those cities, and all the valley, and all the inhabitants of the cities, and what grew on the ground. But his wife looked back (וַתַּבֵּט אִשְׁתּוֹ מֵאַחֲרָיו), and she became a pillar of salt (נְצִיב מֶלַח)."

EXEGESIS:
The phrase וַיהוָה הִמְטִיר... מֵאֵת יְהוָה ("YHWH rained... from YHWH") has been understood by patristic interpreters as a hint of plurality within God—YHWH on earth executes judgment while YHWH in heaven sends it. Justin Martyr and Irenaeus saw here a distinction of divine persons. The elements גָּפְרִית וָאֵשׁ ("sulfur and fire") represent catastrophic reversal—fire from heaven that should bring warmth instead brings destruction; the fertile plain becomes a sulfurous wasteland. The verb הָפַךְ ("to overthrow") becomes technical terminology for Sodom's destruction, used throughout Scripture as the paradigm of divine judgment. Lot's wife's act וַתַּבֵּט מֵאַחֲרָיו ("she looked back from behind him") violated the explicit angelic command (19:17: "Do not look back"). The verb נָבַט ("to look") implies more than a casual glance—it suggests gazing, longing, attachment. Her heart remained in Sodom even as her body fled. The transformation into נְצִיב מֶלַח ("a pillar of salt") has been variously understood—perhaps encasement in volcanic ash or salt spray from the Dead Sea region. Theologically, she becomes a monument to divided loyalty.

CROSS-REFERENCES:

  → Luke 17:28-32
    "Likewise, just as it was in the days of Lot—they were eating and drinking, buying and selling, planting and building, but on the day when Lot went out from Sodom, fire and sulfur rained from heaven (πῦρ καὶ θεῖον ἔβρεξεν ἀπ' οὐρανοῦ) and destroyed them all... Remember Lot's wife (μνημονεύετε τῆς γυναικὸς Λώτ)." Jesus uses Sodom as an eschatological type—His return will come with similar suddenness. His explicit command to "remember Lot's wife" makes her a permanent warning against attachment to the world one is commanded to flee.

  → 2 Peter 2:6
    "By turning the cities of Sodom and Gomorrah to ashes (τεφρώσας) he condemned them to extinction, making them an example (ὑπόδειγμα) of what is going to happen to the ungodly." Peter explicitly designates Sodom as a ὑπόδειγμα ("example/pattern") for future judgment. The destruction was not merely historical but typological—anticipating the final judgment.

  → Jude 7
    "Just as Sodom and Gomorrah and the surrounding cities, which likewise indulged in sexual immorality and pursued unnatural desire (σαρκὸς ἑτέρας), serve as an example by undergoing a punishment of eternal fire (πυρὸς αἰωνίου δίκην)." Jude specifies the sin (sexual perversion) and notes that the judgment serves as δεῖγμα ("sample/example") of eternal fire—the eschatological punishment Sodom's destruction foreshadows.

  → Deuteronomy 29:23
    "The whole land burned out with brimstone and salt (גָּפְרִית וָמֶלַח), nothing sown and nothing growing, where no plant can sprout, an overthrow like that of Sodom and Gomorrah (כְּמַהְפֵּכַת סְדֹם וַעֲמֹרָה)." Moses uses Sodom's destruction as the ultimate comparison for covenant curse. The land becomes as barren as Sodom's remains.

  → Revelation 11:8; 14:10
    "The great city that symbolically is called Sodom (Σόδομα) and Egypt... he also will drink the wine of God's wrath... he will be tormented with fire and sulfur (πυρὶ καὶ θείῳ)." Revelation applies "Sodom" symbolically to rebellious Jerusalem and uses "fire and sulfur" as the consummate image of divine judgment. The Genesis destruction becomes the permanent vocabulary for eschatological wrath.

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
[Genesis 19:29] — GOD REMEMBERED ABRAHAM: DELIVERANCE FOR LOT'S SAKE
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────

וַיְהִ֗י בְּשַׁחֵ֤ת אֱלֹהִים֙ אֶת־עָרֵ֣י הַכִּכָּ֔ר וַיִּזְכֹּ֥ר אֱלֹהִ֖ים אֶת־אַבְרָהָ֑ם וַיְשַׁלַּ֤ח אֶת־ל֙וֹט֙ מִתּ֣וֹךְ הַהֲפֵכָ֔ה בַּהֲפֹ֣ךְ אֶת־הֶעָרִ֔ים אֲשֶׁר־יָשַׁ֥ב בָּהֵ֖ן לֽוֹט׃

"So it was that, when God destroyed (בְּשַׁחֵת אֱלֹהִים) the cities of the valley, God remembered Abraham (וַיִּזְכֹּר אֱלֹהִים אֶת־אַבְרָהָם), and sent Lot out of the midst of the overthrow (מִתּוֹךְ הַהֲפֵכָה) when He overthrew the cities in which Lot had lived."

EXEGESIS:
This verse reveals the theological key to Lot's deliverance: וַיִּזְכֹּר אֱלֹהִים אֶת־אַבְרָהָם ("God remembered Abraham"). Lot was saved not primarily for his own righteousness (though Peter calls him δίκαιος), but because of his connection to Abraham. Divine "remembering" (זָכַר) in Scripture is not mere recollection but covenantal action—God acts on behalf of those He remembers. The same verb pattern appears with Noah (8:1) and with Israel in Egypt (Exodus 2:24). Abraham's intercession, though it did not prevent Sodom's destruction, did secure Lot's rescue. The word הֲפֵכָה ("overthrow") from הָפַךְ becomes the technical term for Sodom's destruction throughout Scripture. The narrative emphasizes that Lot was pulled מִתּוֹךְ ("from the midst of")—at the last moment, from the center of the destruction. He was saved "as through fire" (cf. 1 Corinthians 3:15)—rescued but with total loss of everything he had built in Sodom.

CROSS-REFERENCES:

  → Genesis 8:1
    "But God remembered Noah (וַיִּזְכֹּר אֱלֹהִים אֶת־נֹחַ) and all the beasts and all the livestock that were with him in the ark. And God made a wind blow over the earth, and the waters subsided." The identical phrase—וַיִּזְכֹּר אֱלֹהִים—marks the turning point. Divine remembering initiates salvation action.

  → Exodus 2:24
    "And God heard their groaning, and God remembered his covenant (וַיִּזְכֹּר אֱלֹהִים אֶת־בְּרִיתוֹ) with Abraham, with Isaac, and with Jacob." The Exodus deliverance is grounded in God remembering the Abrahamic covenant. What God promised to Abraham extends to his descendants.

  → Psalm 106:45
    "For their sake he remembered his covenant (וַיִּזְכֹּר לָהֶם בְּרִיתוֹ), and relented according to the abundance of his steadfast love." Divine remembering is the basis for mercy and deliverance throughout Israel's history.

  → 1 Corinthians 3:15
    "If anyone's work is burned up, he will suffer loss, though he himself will be saved, but only as through fire (ὡς διὰ πυρός)." Lot's rescue—saved from fire with total loss of possessions—becomes a type for those who escape judgment but forfeit reward.

═══════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════
                    פרק כ — CHAPTER 20: ABRAHAM AND ABIMELECH
                      נָבִיא — THE PROPHET WHO INTERCEDES FOR KINGS
═══════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
[Genesis 20:7] — ABRAHAM FIRST CALLED PROPHET: INTERCESSORY AUTHORITY
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────

וְעַתָּ֗ה הָשֵׁ֤ב אֵֽשֶׁת־הָאִישׁ֙ כִּֽי־נָבִ֣יא ה֔וּא וְיִתְפַּלֵּ֥ל בַּֽעַדְךָ֖ וֶֽחְיֵ֑ה וְאִם־אֵֽינְךָ֣ מֵשִׁ֔יב דַּ֚ע כִּי־מ֣וֹת תָּמ֔וּת אַתָּ֖ה וְכָל־אֲשֶׁר־לָֽךְ׃

"Now therefore, restore the man's wife (הָשֵׁב אֵשֶׁת־הָאִישׁ), for he is a prophet (כִּי־נָבִיא הוּא), and he will pray for you (וְיִתְפַּלֵּל בַּעַדְךָ), and you shall live. But if you do not restore her, know that you shall surely die (מוֹת תָּמוּת), you and all who are yours."

EXEGESIS:
This is the first occurrence of נָבִיא ("prophet") in Scripture, and it is applied to Abraham. The prophetic role is defined here not primarily as prediction but as intercession—the prophet stands between God and humanity, praying on behalf of others. The phrase וְיִתְפַּלֵּל בַּעַדְךָ ("he will pray for you") establishes intercessory prayer as essential to prophetic ministry. God will not heal Abimelech directly; healing must come through the prophet's mediation. This principle shapes all subsequent biblical prophecy—prophets both speak God's word to people and speak to God on people's behalf. The emphatic מוֹת תָּמוּת ("dying you shall die," i.e., "you shall surely die") echoes the Eden warning (Genesis 2:17), indicating that taking another man's wife incurs capital judgment. Yet restoration and prophetic intercession can avert the sentence. Abraham's role as נָבִיא ("prophet") and intercessor becomes the model for Moses, Samuel, Elijah, and ultimately Christ.

CROSS-REFERENCES:

  → James 5:16-18
    "The prayer of a righteous person has great power as it is working (πολὺ ἰσχύει δέησις δικαίου ἐνεργουμένη). Elijah was a man with a nature like ours, and he prayed fervently that it might not rain, and for three years and six months it did not rain on the earth." James explicitly connects righteous prayer to prophetic authority, citing Elijah as example. Abraham's intercessory ministry establishes the pattern.

  → Job 42:8-10
    "My servant Job shall pray for you, for I will accept his prayer (כִּי אִם־פָּנָיו אֶשָּׂא) not to deal with you according to your folly... And YHWH restored the fortunes of Job, when he had prayed for his friends." Job functions as Abraham—the righteous intercessor whose prayer brings restoration to those under divine displeasure.

  → 1 John 5:16
    "If anyone sees his brother committing a sin not leading to death, he shall ask, and God will give him life (δώσει αὐτῷ ζωήν)." The principle continues in the New Covenant: believers intercede for one another, and God grants life through their prayers.

  → Deuteronomy 18:18
    "I will raise up for them a prophet (נָבִיא) like you from among their brothers. And I will put my words in his mouth." Moses identifies the coming Prophet—ultimately Christ—in the Abrahamic/Mosaic prophetic tradition. Abraham as first נָבִיא establishes the office that culminates in Jesus.

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
[Genesis 20:17-18] — THE PROPHETIC PRAYER: HEALING THROUGH INTERCESSION
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────

וַיִּתְפַּלֵּ֥ל אַבְרָהָ֖ם אֶל־הָאֱלֹהִ֑ים וַיִּרְפָּ֨א אֱלֹהִ֜ים אֶת־אֲבִימֶ֧לֶךְ וְאֶת־אִשְׁתּ֛וֹ וְאַמְהֹתָ֖יו וַיֵּלֵֽדוּ׃
כִּֽי־עָצֹ֤ר עָצַר֙ יְהוָ֔ה בְּעַ֥ד כָּל־רֶ֖חֶם לְבֵ֣ית אֲבִימֶ֑לֶךְ עַל־דְּבַ֥ר שָׂרָ֖ה אֵ֥שֶׁת אַבְרָהָֽם׃

"Abraham prayed to God (וַיִּתְפַּלֵּל אַבְרָהָם אֶל־הָאֱלֹהִים), and God healed Abimelech (וַיִּרְפָּא אֱלֹהִים), and his wife, and his female servants, so that they bore children. For YHWH had completely closed (עָצֹר עָצַר, emphatic) all the wombs of the house of Abimelech because of Sarah (עַל־דְּבַר שָׂרָה), Abraham's wife."

EXEGESIS:
Abraham's prayer fulfills his prophetic role announced in verse 7. The verb וַיִּתְפַּלֵּל (hithpael of פלל, "to pray/intercede") appears here for the first time applied to Abraham. The emphatic עָצֹר עָצַר ("closing He closed," i.e., "completely closed") indicates total barrenness throughout Abimelech's household. The healing וַיִּרְפָּא demonstrates that prophetic intercession has efficacy—the prophet's prayer brings divine healing. This creates a profound irony: Abraham, whose own wife was barren, prays for fertility to be restored to Abimelech's household, and immediately afterward (21:1-2) Sarah conceives Isaac. The closing and opening of wombs belongs exclusively to YHWH's sovereignty (cf. 30:2, 22; 1 Samuel 1:5-6). Abraham's intercession for a pagan king demonstrates that prophetic ministry extends beyond Israel—blessing comes through Abraham to all nations, even those who unwittingly sin.

CROSS-REFERENCES:

  → Numbers 12:13
    "And Moses cried to YHWH, 'O God, please heal her (אֵל נָא רְפָא נָא לָהּ)!'" Moses intercedes for Miriam's leprosy in the same pattern—prophetic prayer brings divine healing. The prophet mediates between God's judgment and the afflicted.

  → 2 Kings 5:11
    "I thought that he would surely come out to me and stand and call upon the name of YHWH his God (וְקָרָא בְּשֵׁם־יְהוָה אֱלֹהָיו), and wave his hand over the place and cure the leprosy." Naaman expected prophetic healing through Elisha's intercession. The pattern established with Abraham continues: prophets pray, God heals.

  → James 5:14-15
    "Is anyone among you sick? Let him call for the elders of the church, and let them pray over him... and the prayer of faith (ἡ εὐχὴ τῆς πίστεως) will save the one who is sick, and the Lord will raise him up." The New Covenant continues the pattern: authorized intercessors pray, God heals. The prophetic office extends to church elders.

═══════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════
              פרק כא — CHAPTER 21: THE BIRTH OF ISAAC AND HAGAR SENT AWAY
                       יִצְחָק — LAUGHTER BORN OF PROMISE
═══════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════

═══════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════
[Genesis 21:1-3] — YHWH VISITS SARAH: THE CHILD OF PROMISE IS BORN
═══════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════

וַיהוָ֛ה פָּקַ֥ד אֶת־שָׂרָ֖ה כַּאֲשֶׁ֣ר אָמָ֑ר וַיַּ֧עַשׂ יְהוָ֛ה לְשָׂרָ֖ה כַּאֲשֶׁ֥ר דִּבֵּֽר׃
וַתַּ֩הַר֩ וַתֵּ֨לֶד שָׂרָ֧ה לְאַבְרָהָ֛ם בֵּ֖ן לִזְקֻנָ֑יו לַמּוֹעֵ֕ד אֲשֶׁר־דִּבֶּ֥ר אֹת֖וֹ אֱלֹהִֽים׃
וַיִּקְרָ֨א אַבְרָהָ֜ם אֶת־שֶׁם־בְּנ֧וֹ הַנּֽוֹלַד־ל֛וֹ אֲשֶׁר־יָלְדָה־לּ֥וֹ שָׂרָ֖ה יִצְחָֽק׃

"And YHWH visited Sarah (וַיהוָה פָּקַד אֶת־שָׂרָה) as He had said, and YHWH did to Sarah as He had spoken. Sarah conceived and bore Abraham a son in his old age (בֵּן לִזְקֻנָיו), at the appointed time (לַמּוֹעֵד) of which God had spoken to him. Abraham called the name of his son who was born to him, whom Sarah bore to him, Isaac (יִצְחָק)."

EXEGESIS:
The narrative emphasizes divine initiative and faithfulness: YHWH פָּקַד ("visited")—the same verb used for God's redemptive intervention in Exodus 3:16 and 4:31. The double emphasis כַּאֲשֶׁר אָמָר... כַּאֲשֶׁר דִּבֵּר ("as He said... as He spoke") underscores that God fulfills His word exactly. The phrase לַמּוֹעֵד ("at the appointed time") recalls 18:14—God keeps His schedule precisely. Isaac is born לִזְקֻנָיו ("in his [Abraham's] old age")—the miraculous nature of the birth is emphasized. The name יִצְחָק ("he laughs/will laugh") transforms the skeptical laughter of 17:17 and 18:12 into joy. What began as incredulous response to impossible promise becomes the name of fulfillment. The child of promise is called "Laughter"—every mention of his name recalls that God kept His word against all natural expectation. Isaac's birth becomes the paradigm for all salvation: impossible humanly, accomplished divinely, received by faith.

CROSS-REFERENCES:

  → Galatians 4:22-23, 28
    "For it is written that Abraham had two sons, one by a slave woman and one by a free woman. But the son of the slave was born according to the flesh (κατὰ σάρκα), while the son of the free woman was born through promise (δι' ἐπαγγελίας)... Now you, brothers, like Isaac, are children of promise." Paul establishes the fundamental distinction: Ishmael = flesh; Isaac = promise. Believers are Isaac's spiritual children.

  → Romans 9:9
    "For this is what the promise said: 'About this time next year I will return, and Sarah shall have a son (κατὰ τὸν καιρὸν τοῦτον ἐλεύσομαι καὶ ἔσται τῇ Σάρρᾳ υἱός).'" Paul quotes Genesis 18:10, emphasizing divine sovereignty in fulfillment. God accomplishes what He promises at His appointed time.

  → Hebrews 11:11-12
    "By faith Sarah herself received power to conceive (δύναμιν εἰς καταβολὴν σπέρματος ἔλαβεν), even when she was past the age, since she considered him faithful who had promised. Therefore from one man, and him as good as dead, were born descendants as many as the stars of heaven." Sarah's faith, despite initial doubt, is celebrated. Divine faithfulness overcame human impossibility.

  → Luke 1:36-37, 45
    "And behold, your relative Elizabeth in her old age has also conceived a son... For nothing will be impossible with God (οὐκ ἀδυνατήσει παρὰ τοῦ θεοῦ πᾶν ῥῆμα)... Blessed is she who believed that there would be a fulfillment of what was spoken to her from the Lord." Elizabeth's late-life conception parallels Sarah's. Gabriel explicitly connects Mary's virgin conception to the Sarah precedent.

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
[Genesis 21:9-10] — FLESH PERSECUTES SPIRIT: SARAH'S PROPHETIC DEMAND
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────

וַתֵּ֨רֶא שָׂרָ֜ה אֶֽת־בֶּן־הָגָ֧ר הַמִּצְרִ֛ית אֲשֶׁר־יָלְדָ֥ה לְאַבְרָהָ֖ם מְצַחֵֽק׃
וַתֹּ֙אמֶר֙ לְאַבְרָהָ֔ם גָּרֵ֛שׁ הָאָמָ֥ה הַזֹּ֖את וְאֶת־בְּנָ֑הּ כִּ֣י לֹ֤א יִירַשׁ֙ בֶּן־הָאָמָ֣ה הַזֹּ֔את עִם־בְּנִ֖י עִם־יִצְחָֽק׃

"But Sarah saw the son of Hagar the Egyptian, whom she had borne to Abraham, laughing/mocking (מְצַחֵק). So she said to Abraham, 'Cast out (גָּרֵשׁ) this slave woman and her son, for the son of this slave woman shall not be heir (לֹא יִירַשׁ) with my son Isaac.'"

EXEGESIS:
The verb מְצַחֵק (piel participle of צָחַק, "to laugh") creates a wordplay on Isaac's name (יִצְחָק)—Ishmael is "Isaac-ing," mockingly imitating or derisively playing upon the name and identity of the promised son. The LXX adds παίζοντα μετὰ Ἰσαὰκ ("playing with Isaac"), and Paul in Galatians 4:29 interprets this as ἐδίωκεν ("persecuted"). Sarah's demand גָּרֵשׁ ("drive out/divorce") is the same verb used for divorce and expulsion. Her words לֹא יִירַשׁ ("shall not inherit") touch the heart of the matter: who are the true heirs? Paul quotes Sarah's words directly (Galatians 4:30) as Scripture's verdict on the relationship between flesh and promise. Though Sarah's motivation may have included jealousy, God confirms her demand (v. 12)—indicating that her words carry prophetic authority. The separation of Ishmael from Isaac becomes a type of the necessary separation between law and grace, flesh and Spirit.

CROSS-REFERENCES:

  → Galatians 4:29-31
    "But just as at that time he who was born according to the flesh persecuted (ἐδίωκεν) him who was born according to the Spirit, so also it is now. But what does the Scripture say? 'Cast out the slave woman and her son (ἔκβαλε τὴν παιδίσκην καὶ τὸν υἱὸν αὐτῆς), for the son of the slave woman shall not inherit (οὐ μὴ κληρονομήσει) with the son of the free woman.' So, brothers, we are not children of the slave but of the free woman." Paul directly quotes Sarah's words as authoritative Scripture, interpreting the historical event as paradigmatic: those born of flesh always persecute those born of Spirit.

  → Romans 9:7-8
    "And not all are children of Abraham because they are his offspring, but 'Through Isaac shall your offspring be named.' This means that it is not the children of the flesh (τέκνα τῆς σαρκός) who are the children of God, but the children of the promise (τέκνα τῆς ἐπαγγελίας) are counted as offspring." Paul establishes the principle: physical descent from Abraham does not guarantee covenant membership. Spiritual birth through promise supersedes natural birth.

  → John 8:39-40
    "They answered him, 'Abraham is our father.' Jesus said to them, 'If you were Abraham's children, you would be doing the works Abraham did, but now you seek to kill me.'" Jesus confronts those who claim Abrahamic descent but act like Ishmael—persecuting the true Seed of promise. Physical lineage without faith-obedience is meaningless.

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
[Genesis 21:12] — THROUGH ISAAC YOUR SEED SHALL BE NAMED: DIVINE CONFIRMATION
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────

וַיֹּ֨אמֶר אֱלֹהִ֜ים אֶל־אַבְרָהָ֗ם אַל־יֵרַ֤ע בְּעֵינֶ֙יךָ֙ עַל־הַנַּ֣עַר וְעַל־אֲמָתֶ֔ךָ כֹּל֩ אֲשֶׁ֨ר תֹּאמַ֥ר אֵלֶ֛יךָ שָׂרָ֖ה שְׁמַ֣ע בְּקֹלָ֑הּ כִּ֣י בְיִצְחָ֔ק יִקָּרֵ֥א לְךָ֖ זָֽרַע׃

"But God said to Abraham, 'Be not displeased because of the boy and because of your slave woman (אַל־יֵרַע בְּעֵינֶיךָ). Whatever Sarah says to you, listen to her voice (שְׁמַע בְּקֹלָהּ), for through Isaac shall your offspring be named (כִּי בְיִצְחָק יִקָּרֵא לְךָ זָרַע).'"

EXEGESIS:
God's confirmation of Sarah's demand establishes a crucial principle: the covenant line runs exclusively through Isaac. The phrase בְיִצְחָק יִקָּרֵא לְךָ זָרַע ("through Isaac your seed shall be called/named") becomes a proof-text for Pauline theology of election. The verb יִקָּרֵא (niphal of קָרָא) means "shall be called/named"—suggesting identity and recognition. Abraham's true seed will be identified through Isaac, not Ishmael. God's command שְׁמַע בְּקֹלָהּ ("listen to her voice") reverses the earlier pattern where listening to Sarah led to the Hagar disaster (16:2). Now, in this case, Sarah speaks prophetically and Abraham is to obey. The principle established: not all physical descendants are covenant heirs; election determines inheritance. This verse becomes foundational for Romans 9's theology of divine sovereignty in choosing the objects of His mercy.

CROSS-REFERENCES:

  → Romans 9:7-8
    "And not all are children of Abraham because they are his offspring, but 'Through Isaac shall your offspring be named (ἐν Ἰσαὰκ κληθήσεταί σοι σπέρμα).' This means that it is not the children of the flesh who are the children of God, but the children of the promise are counted as offspring." Paul quotes this verse directly to establish that divine election, not physical descent, determines who belongs to God's people.

  → Hebrews 11:17-18
    "By faith Abraham, when he was tested, offered up Isaac, and he who had received the promises was in the act of offering up his only son, of whom it was said, 'Through Isaac shall your offspring be named (ἐν Ἰσαὰκ κληθήσεταί σοι σπέρμα).'" The author quotes this verse to heighten the tension of the Akedah—Abraham was willing to sacrifice the very son through whom the promise must flow.

  → Galatians 3:16
    "Now the promises were made to Abraham and to his offspring (σπέρματι). It does not say, 'And to offsprings (σπέρμασιν),' as of many, but as of one, 'And to your offspring (σπέρματί),' who is Christ." Paul traces the singular "seed" through Isaac to its ultimate referent: Christ. The covenant line Isaac→Jacob→Judah→David terminates in Jesus.

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
[Genesis 21:17-19] — GOD HEARS ISHMAEL: GRACE BEYOND THE COVENANT LINE
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────

וַיִּשְׁמַ֣ע אֱלֹהִים֮ אֶת־ק֣וֹל הַנַּעַר֒ וַיִּקְרָ֨א מַלְאַ֤ךְ אֱלֹהִים֙ אֶל־הָגָ֔ר מִן־הַשָּׁמַ֔יִם וַיֹּ֥אמֶר לָ֖הּ מַה־לָּ֣ךְ הָגָ֑ר אַל־תִּ֣ירְאִ֔י כִּֽי־שָׁמַ֧ע אֱלֹהִ֛ים אֶל־ק֥וֹל הַנַּ֖עַר בַּאֲשֶׁ֥ר הוּא־שָֽׁם׃
ק֚וּמִי שְׂאִ֣י אֶת־הַנַּ֔עַר וְהַחֲזִ֥יקִי אֶת־יָדֵ֖ךְ בּ֑וֹ כִּֽי־לְג֥וֹי גָּד֖וֹל אֲשִׂימֶֽנּוּ׃
וַיִּפְקַ֤ח אֱלֹהִים֙ אֶת־עֵינֶ֔יהָ וַתֵּ֖רֶא בְּאֵ֣ר מָ֑יִם

"And God heard the voice of the boy (וַיִּשְׁמַע אֱלֹהִים אֶת־קוֹל הַנַּעַר), and the angel of God called to Hagar from heaven and said to her, 'What troubles you, Hagar? Fear not (אַל־תִּירְאִי), for God has heard the voice of the boy where he is (בַּאֲשֶׁר הוּא־שָׁם). Rise up, lift up the boy, and hold him fast with your hand, for I will make him into a great nation (לְגוֹי גָּדוֹל).' Then God opened her eyes (וַיִּפְקַח אֱלֹהִים אֶת־עֵינֶיהָ), and she saw a well of water (בְּאֵר מָיִם)."

EXEGESIS:
The phrase וַיִּשְׁמַע אֱלֹהִים ("God heard") fulfills Ishmael's name-prophecy: יִשְׁמָעֵאל means "God hears." Even outside the covenant line, God attends to the afflicted. The formula בַּאֲשֶׁר הוּא־שָׁם ("where he is") emphasizes that God meets people in their actual circumstances—not where they ought to be, but where they are. The promise לְגוֹי גָּדוֹל ("a great nation") echoes the Abrahamic blessing (12:2)—Ishmael receives national promise though not covenant promise. The divine opening of eyes (וַיִּפְקַח אֱלֹהִים אֶת־עֵינֶיהָ) reveals that the well was always there—Hagar lacked perception, not provision. This anticipates the spiritual principle: divine provision often awaits opened eyes. The בְּאֵר מָיִם ("well of water") in the wilderness becomes a type of God's provision for those wandering outside the promised land—grace extends beyond covenant boundaries.

CROSS-REFERENCES:

  → Genesis 16:11
    "The angel of YHWH said to her, 'You shall call his name Ishmael (יִשְׁמָעֵאל), for YHWH has heard (כִּי־שָׁמַע יְהוָה) your affliction.'" The naming is now fulfilled—God continues to "hear" (שָׁמַע) Ishmael throughout his life. The theophoric name proves prophetically accurate.

  → Psalm 34:6, 17
    "This poor man cried, and YHWH heard him (שָׁמַע יְהוָה) and saved him out of all his troubles... The righteous cry out, and YHWH hears (שָׁמַע יְהוָה)." The pattern of divine responsiveness to human cry pervades Scripture. God's hearing initiates deliverance.

  → 2 Kings 6:17
    "Then YHWH opened the eyes of the servant (וַיִּפְקַח יְהוָה אֶת־עֵינֵי הַנַּעַר), and he saw, and behold, the mountain was full of horses and chariots of fire." The same verb וַיִּפְקַח—God opens eyes to perceive what was always present. Divine provision precedes human perception.

  → John 4:14
    "Whoever drinks of the water that I will give him will never be thirsty again. The water that I will give him will become in him a spring of water (πηγὴ ὕδατος) welling up to eternal life." The wilderness well points to Christ's living water—provision for those dying of spiritual thirst.

═══════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════
              פרק כב — CHAPTER 22: THE BINDING OF ISAAC (THE AKEDAH)
                     עֲקֵדַת יִצְחָק — THE SUPREME TYPE OF CALVARY
═══════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════

═══════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════
[Genesis 22:1-2] — TAKE YOUR SON, YOUR ONLY SON: THE TEST OF ULTIMATE FAITH
═══════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════

וַיְהִ֗י אַחַר֙ הַדְּבָרִ֣ים הָאֵ֔לֶּה וְהָ֣אֱלֹהִ֔ים נִסָּ֖ה אֶת־אַבְרָהָ֑ם וַיֹּ֣אמֶר אֵלָ֔יו אַבְרָהָ֖ם וַיֹּ֥אמֶר הִנֵּֽנִי׃
וַיֹּ֡אמֶר קַח־נָ֠א אֶת־בִּנְךָ֨ אֶת־יְחִֽידְךָ֤ אֲשֶׁר־אָהַ֙בְתָּ֙ אֶת־יִצְחָ֔ק וְלֶךְ־לְךָ֔ אֶל־אֶ֖רֶץ הַמֹּרִיָּ֑ה וְהַעֲלֵ֤הוּ שָׁם֙ לְעֹלָ֔ה עַ֚ל אַחַ֣ד הֶהָרִ֔ים אֲשֶׁ֖ר אֹמַ֥ר אֵלֶֽיךָ׃

"After these things God tested Abraham (וְהָאֱלֹהִים נִסָּה אֶת־אַבְרָהָם), and said to him, 'Abraham!' And he said, 'Here I am (הִנֵּנִי).' He said, 'Take your son (קַח־נָא אֶת־בִּנְךָ), your only son (אֶת־יְחִידְךָ), whom you love (אֲשֶׁר־אָהַבְתָּ), Isaac, and go (וְלֶךְ־לְךָ) to the land of Moriah (אֶרֶץ הַמֹּרִיָּה), and offer him there as a burnt offering (לְעֹלָה) on one of the mountains of which I shall tell you.'"

EXEGESIS:
This chapter is universally recognized as the supreme typological prefiguration of Calvary in the Hebrew Scriptures. The verb נִסָּה ("tested/proved") indicates God's purpose: not to destroy Abraham's faith but to prove and perfect it. The fourfold identification of Isaac—בִּנְךָ ("your son"), יְחִידְךָ ("your only one"), אֲשֶׁר־אָהַבְתָּ ("whom you love"), יִצְחָק ("Isaac")—builds emotional intensity with each phrase, culminating in the beloved name. The word יָחִיד ("only/unique") is crucial—the LXX renders it ἀγαπητόν ("beloved"), which appears in the NT for Christ (Matthew 3:17). Isaac is the first person in Scripture explicitly said to be "loved" (אָהַב)—and he is to be sacrificed. The phrase וְלֶךְ־לְךָ ("go yourself") echoes Abraham's original call (12:1: לֶךְ־לְךָ), creating an inclusio: his journey of faith began with leaving and now reaches its climax with offering. The אֶרֶץ הַמֹּרִיָּה ("land of Moriah") later becomes the site of Solomon's Temple (2 Chronicles 3:1) and, according to Christian tradition, the location of Golgotha—the place where the Father would offer His beloved Son.

CROSS-REFERENCES:

  → John 3:16
    "For God so loved the world, that he gave his only Son (τὸν υἱὸν τὸν μονογενῆ), that whoever believes in him should not perish but have eternal life." The Father's sacrifice of His beloved, only Son fulfills what Abraham enacted typologically. The phrase τὸν υἱὸν τὸν μονογενῆ echoes יְחִידְךָ אֲשֶׁר־אָהַבְתָּ.

  → Romans 8:32
    "He who did not spare his own Son (τοῦ ἰδίου υἱοῦ οὐκ ἐφείσατο) but gave him up for us all, how will he not also with him graciously give us all things?" Paul's language directly echoes Genesis 22:12, 16 (לֹא חָשַׂכְתָּ אֶת־בִּנְךָ, "you did not withhold your son"). What Abraham was willing to do, God actually did.

  → Hebrews 11:17-19
    "By faith Abraham, when he was tested (πειραζόμενος), offered up Isaac, and he who had received the promises was in the act of offering up his only son (τὸν μονογενῆ), of whom it was said, 'Through Isaac shall your offspring be named.' He considered that God was able even to raise him from the dead (ἐκ νεκρῶν ἐγείρειν), from which, figuratively speaking (ἐν παραβολῇ), he did receive him back." The author interprets the Akedah as a resurrection type: Isaac's figurative death and restoration prefigure Christ's actual death and resurrection.

  → James 2:21-22
    "Was not Abraham our father justified by works when he offered up his son Isaac on the altar? You see that faith was active along with his works, and faith was completed (ἐτελειώθη) by his works." James emphasizes that Abraham's faith found its τέλος (completion/perfection) in the Akedah. This was the faith that justified him in chapter 15, now demonstrated and perfected.

  → 2 Chronicles 3:1
    "Then Solomon began to build the house of YHWH in Jerusalem on Mount Moriah (בְּהַר הַמּוֹרִיָּה), where YHWH had appeared to David his father." The Temple is built on the very mountain where Abraham offered Isaac. Later tradition identifies this with Golgotha—the place of sacrifice throughout redemptive history.

═══════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════
[Genesis 22:6-8] — ISAAC CARRIES THE WOOD: "GOD WILL PROVIDE HIMSELF THE LAMB"
═══════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════

וַיִּקַּ֨ח אַבְרָהָ֜ם אֶת־עֲצֵ֣י הָעֹלָ֗ה וַיָּ֙שֶׂם֙ עַל־יִצְחָ֣ק בְּנ֔וֹ וַיִּקַּ֣ח בְּיָד֔וֹ אֶת־הָאֵ֖שׁ וְאֶת־הַֽמַּאֲכֶ֑לֶת וַיֵּלְכ֥וּ שְׁנֵיהֶ֖ם יַחְדָּֽו׃
וַיֹּ֨אמֶר יִצְחָ֜ק אֶל־אַבְרָהָ֤ם אָבִיו֙ וַיֹּ֣אמֶר אָבִ֔י וַיֹּ֖אמֶר הִנֶּ֣נִּֽי בְנִ֑י וַיֹּ֗אמֶר הִנֵּ֤ה הָאֵשׁ֙ וְהָ֣עֵצִ֔ים וְאַיֵּ֥ה הַשֶּׂ֖ה לְעֹלָֽה׃
וַיֹּ֙אמֶר֙ אַבְרָהָ֔ם אֱלֹהִ֞ים יִרְאֶה־לּ֥וֹ הַשֶּׂ֛ה לְעֹלָ֖ה בְּנִ֑י וַיֵּלְכ֥וּ שְׁנֵיהֶ֖ם יַחְדָּֽו׃

"And Abraham took the wood of the burnt offering (עֲצֵי הָעֹלָה) and laid it upon Isaac his son (וַיָּשֶׂם עַל־יִצְחָק בְּנוֹ), and he took in his hand the fire and the knife (הָאֵשׁ וְאֶת־הַמַּאֲכֶלֶת); and they went, both of them together (וַיֵּלְכוּ שְׁנֵיהֶם יַחְדָּו). And Isaac spoke to Abraham his father and said, 'My father!' (אָבִי) And he said, 'Here I am, my son' (הִנֶּנִּי בְנִי). And he said, 'Behold the fire and the wood, but where is the lamb for a burnt offering?' (וְאַיֵּה הַשֶּׂה לְעֹלָה). And Abraham said, 'God will provide Himself the lamb for a burnt offering, my son' (אֱלֹהִים יִרְאֶה־לּוֹ הַשֶּׂה לְעֹלָה בְּנִי); and they went, both of them together."

EXEGESIS:

This passage contains perhaps the most profound typological tableau in all Scripture: Isaac, the son of promise, carrying upon his own shoulders the wood (עֵצִים) of his sacrifice up Mount Moriah—precisely as Christ would carry His cross to Golgotha. The Fathers universally identified this moment as the clearest Old Testament prefiguration of the Via Dolorosa. The phrase "they went, both of them together" (וַיֵּלְכוּ שְׁנֵיהֶם יַחְדָּו) appears twice, emphasizing the unity of will between father and son in the sacrificial procession—mirroring the eternal harmony of Father and Son in the work of redemption. Isaac's poignant question "Where is the lamb?" (וְאַיֵּה הַשֶּׂה) echoes through the ages until John the Baptist answers definitively: "Behold, the Lamb of God!" Abraham's response is prophetic oracle: "God will provide Himself the lamb" (אֱלֹהִים יִרְאֶה־לּוֹ הַשֶּׂה). The Hebrew permits the translation "God will provide Himself as the lamb"—a stunning declaration that God Himself would become the sacrifice. The verb יִרְאֶה (yir'eh, "will see/provide") becomes the name of the mountain and the theological foundation of substitutionary atonement. St. Melito of Sardis explicitly taught that Isaac carrying the wood typified Christ carrying the cross, and that Abraham's prophecy was fulfilled when God provided not merely a lamb but His own Son.

CROSS-REFERENCES:

  → John 19:17
    "So they took Jesus, and he went out, bearing his own cross (βαστάζων ἑαυτῷ τὸν σταυρόν), to the place called The Place of a Skull, which in Aramaic is called Golgotha." The typological correspondence is unmistakable: as Isaac bore the עֲצֵי הָעֹלָה (wood of the burnt offering) on his own shoulders up Moriah, so Christ bore τὸν σταυρόν (the cross) on His shoulders up Golgotha. Both are beloved sons, both carry the instrument of their own sacrifice, both ascend a mount outside Jerusalem. St. Ephrem the Syrian noted that Isaac was not merely carrying wood but prophetically enacting Christ's passion centuries before its fulfillment.

  → John 1:29
    "The next day he saw Jesus coming toward him, and said, 'Behold, the Lamb of God (ἴδε ὁ ἀμνὸς τοῦ θεοῦ), who takes away the sin of the world!'" John the Baptist's proclamation is the definitive answer to Isaac's question "Where is the lamb?" Abraham's prophetic utterance "God will provide Himself the lamb" finds its fulfillment not in the ram caught in the thicket (which was merely a temporary type) but in Christ, the true Lamb foreordained before the foundation of the world. The word ἀμνός (lamb) appears in the LXX as πρόβατον for the Akedah passage, but John uses ἀμνός—the Passover lamb term—connecting Moriah to the Exodus and both to Calvary.

  → 1 Peter 1:19-20
    "But with the precious blood of Christ, like that of a lamb without blemish or spot (ἀμνοῦ ἀμώμου καὶ ἀσπίλου). He was foreknown before the foundation of the world (πρὸ καταβολῆς κόσμου) but was made manifest in the last times for the sake of you." The Lamb was "provided" not as an afterthought but from eternity—Abraham's prophecy touched upon the eternal decree. The phrase πρὸ καταβολῆς κόσμου reveals that when Abraham said "God will provide," he glimpsed by faith the pre-temporal counsel of the Trinity whereby the Son would offer Himself as the spotless sacrifice.

  → Revelation 5:6, 12
    "And between the throne and the four living creatures and among the elders I saw a Lamb standing, as though it had been slain (ἀρνίον ἑστηκὸς ὡς ἐσφαγμένον)... 'Worthy is the Lamb who was slain (τὸ ἀρνίον τὸ ἐσφαγμένον).'" The Lamb whom Abraham prophesied now stands at the center of heavenly worship—slain yet standing, dead yet risen. The Akedah is transformed in Revelation: Isaac was bound but released; Christ was slain but stands eternally victorious. The wood Isaac carried becomes the throne; the mountain of sacrifice becomes the mountain of glory.

  → Hebrews 11:17-19
    "By faith Abraham, when he was tested, offered up Isaac... He considered that God was able even to raise him from the dead (ἐκ νεκρῶν ἐγείρειν), from which, figuratively speaking (ἐν παραβολῇ), he did receive him back." The phrase ἐν παραβολῇ confirms the typological reading: Abraham received Isaac back "in a figure/parable"—meaning the physical restoration of Isaac pictured the resurrection of Christ. Abraham's faith in resurrection made him willing to proceed, knowing that God's promise of descendants through Isaac required Isaac's survival or revival.

═══════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════
[Genesis 22:9-10] — THE BINDING: ISAAC BOUND UPON THE ALTAR OF SACRIFICE
═══════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════

וַיָּבֹ֗אוּ אֶֽל־הַמָּקוֹם֮ אֲשֶׁ֣ר אָֽמַר־ל֣וֹ הָאֱלֹהִים֒ וַיִּ֨בֶן שָׁ֤ם אַבְרָהָם֙ אֶת־הַמִּזְבֵּ֔חַ וַיַּֽעֲרֹ֖ךְ אֶת־הָעֵצִ֑ים וַיַּֽעֲקֹד֙ אֶת־יִצְחָ֣ק בְּנ֔וֹ וַיָּ֤שֶׂם אֹתוֹ֙ עַל־הַמִּזְבֵּ֔חַ מִמַּ֖עַל לָעֵצִֽים׃
וַיִּשְׁלַ֤ח אַבְרָהָם֙ אֶת־יָד֔וֹ וַיִּקַּ֖ח אֶת־הַֽמַּאֲכֶ֑לֶת לִשְׁחֹ֖ט אֶת־בְּנֽוֹ׃

"And they came to the place (אֶל־הַמָּקוֹם) which God had told him of. And Abraham built there the altar (וַיִּבֶן שָׁם אַבְרָהָם אֶת־הַמִּזְבֵּחַ), and arranged the wood (וַיַּעֲרֹךְ אֶת־הָעֵצִים), and bound Isaac his son (וַיַּעֲקֹד אֶת־יִצְחָק בְּנוֹ), and laid him on the altar upon the wood (וַיָּשֶׂם אֹתוֹ עַל־הַמִּזְבֵּחַ מִמַּעַל לָעֵצִים). And Abraham stretched forth his hand (וַיִּשְׁלַח אַבְרָהָם אֶת־יָדוֹ), and took the knife to slay his son (וַיִּקַּח אֶת־הַמַּאֲכֶלֶת לִשְׁחֹט אֶת־בְּנוֹ)."

EXEGESIS:

The verb וַיַּעֲקֹד (wayya'aqod, "and he bound") gives this entire narrative its name: the עֲקֵדָה (Akedah, "the Binding"). This unique word appears nowhere else in Scripture in this form, marking this event as singular in sacred history. The careful, deliberate sequence—building, arranging, binding, laying—reveals Abraham's unwavering obedience despite the agony of each action. Isaac's silence throughout is remarkable: Jewish tradition notes that Isaac was likely in his late twenties or thirties, fully capable of resisting his elderly father, yet he submitted voluntarily. This willing submission (Greek: ὑπακοή) prefigures Christ's obedience unto death on the cross. The phrase "upon the wood" (מִמַּעַל לָעֵצִים) places Isaac directly upon the instrument of sacrifice—as Christ was placed upon the cross-beams of Golgotha. The מַאֲכֶלֶת (ma'akelet, "knife") is the sacrificial slaughtering knife, emphasizing that this was a genuine priestly act, not symbolic theater. St. John Chrysostom marveled that Abraham's hand did not tremble, his soul did not waver, demonstrating faith that penetrated beyond visible circumstances to the promises of God. The altar built by Abraham's own hands would receive his own son—just as the Father would deliver His own Son to the cross prepared by human malice.

CROSS-REFERENCES:

  → Philippians 2:8
    "And being found in human form, he humbled himself by becoming obedient to the point of death (γενόμενος ὑπήκοος μέχρι θανάτου), even death on a cross (θανάτου δὲ σταυροῦ)." Isaac's willing submission to be bound—when he could have overpowered his aged father—prefigures the κένωσις (self-emptying) of Christ who, though possessing divine power, submitted to crucifixion. The phrase ὑπήκοος μέχρι θανάτου (obedient unto death) is the essence of both Isaac's and Christ's sacrifice: voluntary self-offering that held nothing back. The altar becomes the cross; the binding foreshadows the nailing.

  → Isaiah 53:7
    "He was oppressed, and he was afflicted, yet he opened not his mouth (וְהוּא נַעֲנֶה וְלֹא יִפְתַּח־פִּיו); like a lamb (כַּשֶּׂה) that is led to the slaughter, and like a sheep that before its shearers is silent, so he opened not his mouth." Isaac's silence before Abraham parallels the Suffering Servant's silence before His accusers. Jewish midrash records that Isaac asked to be bound lest he flinch and render the sacrifice invalid—a willing participant in his own sacrifice. Christ likewise "opened not his mouth" before Pilate and Herod, accepting the binding of human chains and the binding of the cross.

  → Hebrews 9:14
    "How much more will the blood of Christ, who through the eternal Spirit (διὰ πνεύματος αἰωνίου) offered himself without blemish to God (ἑαυτὸν προσήνεγκεν ἄμωμον τῷ θεῷ), purify our conscience from dead works to serve the living God." The key phrase ἑαυτὸν προσήνεγκεν (offered Himself) reveals that Christ's sacrifice, like Isaac's binding, was voluntary self-offering. The διὰ πνεύματος αἰωνίου indicates that this offering transcended time—connecting the eternal decree glimpsed at Moriah to its fulfillment at Calvary.

  → Romans 8:32
    "He who did not spare his own Son (τοῦ ἰδίου υἱοῦ οὐκ ἐφείσατο) but gave him up for us all (ὑπὲρ ἡμῶν πάντων παρέδωκεν αὐτόν), how will he not also with him graciously give us all things?" Paul's language deliberately echoes Genesis 22:12 LXX: οὐκ ἐφείσω τοῦ υἱοῦ σου τοῦ ἀγαπητοῦ ("you did not spare your beloved son"). But the crucial difference: Abraham was stopped; the Father was not. Abraham's hand was stayed by the angel; the Father's hand delivered Christ to death. The type is exceeded by the antitype.

  → John 10:17-18
    "For this reason the Father loves me, because I lay down my life (ἐγὼ τίθημι τὴν ψυχήν μου) that I may take it up again. No one takes it from me, but I lay it down of my own accord (ἀπ' ἐμαυτοῦ τίθημι αὐτήν)." Christ explicitly declares the voluntary nature of His death, paralleling Isaac's willing submission to be bound. The phrase ἀπ' ἐμαυτοῦ (of myself) emphasizes autonomous choice—Isaac chose not to resist; Christ chose to submit. Both demonstrate that true sacrifice must be voluntary.

═══════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════
[Genesis 22:11-13] — THE RAM IN THE THICKET: DIVINE SUBSTITUTION ESTABLISHED
═══════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════

וַיִּקְרָ֨א אֵלָ֜יו מַלְאַ֤ךְ יְהוָה֙ מִן־הַשָּׁמַ֔יִם וַיֹּ֖אמֶר אַבְרָהָ֣ם ׀ אַבְרָהָ֑ם וַיֹּ֖אמֶר הִנֵּֽנִי׃
וַיֹּ֗אמֶר אַל־תִּשְׁלַ֤ח יָֽדְךָ֙ אֶל־הַנַּ֔עַר וְאַל־תַּ֥עַשׂ ל֖וֹ מְא֑וּמָה כִּ֣י ׀ עַתָּ֣ה יָדַ֗עְתִּי כִּֽי־יְרֵ֤א אֱלֹהִים֙ אַ֔תָּה וְלֹ֥א חָשַׂ֛כְתָּ אֶת־בִּנְךָ֥ אֶת־יְחִידְךָ֖ מִמֶּֽנִּי׃
וַיִּשָּׂ֨א אַבְרָהָ֜ם אֶת־עֵינָ֗יו וַיַּרְא֙ וְהִנֵּה־אַ֔יִל אַחַ֕ר נֶאֱחַ֥ז בַּסְּבַ֖ךְ בְּקַרְנָ֑יו וַיֵּ֤לֶךְ אַבְרָהָם֙ וַיִּקַּ֣ח אֶת־הָאַ֔יִל וַיַּעֲלֵ֥הוּ לְעֹלָ֖ה תַּ֥חַת בְּנֽוֹ׃

"And the Angel of YHWH (מַלְאַךְ יְהוָה) called to him from heaven and said, 'Abraham! Abraham!' And he said, 'Here I am' (הִנֵּנִי). And He said, 'Do not stretch out your hand against the boy (אַל־תִּשְׁלַח יָדְךָ אֶל־הַנַּעַר), and do not do anything to him; for now I know that you fear God (יְרֵא אֱלֹהִים), since you have not withheld your son, your only son, from Me (וְלֹא חָשַׂכְתָּ אֶת־בִּנְךָ אֶת־יְחִידְךָ מִמֶּנִּי).' And Abraham lifted up his eyes and looked, and behold, behind him was a ram (אַיִל) caught in the thicket by his horns (נֶאֱחַז בַּסְּבַךְ בְּקַרְנָיו). And Abraham went and took the ram, and offered him up as a burnt offering instead of his son (תַּחַת בְּנוֹ)."

EXEGESIS:

The double call "Abraham! Abraham!" (אַבְרָהָם אַבְרָהָם) signals urgency from heaven—the same reduplicated address used at the burning bush ("Moses! Moses!") and later ("Samuel! Samuel!"). The phrase "you have not withheld" (לֹא חָשַׂכְתָּ) uses the verb חָשַׂךְ (chasakh), meaning to hold back or spare—this exact terminology becomes crucial in Romans 8:32 where Paul declares that God "did not spare" (οὐκ ἐφείσατο) His own Son. The Angel speaks in first person: "from Me" (מִמֶּנִּי)—identifying the מַלְאַךְ יְהוָה as the pre-incarnate Logos, the Second Person of the Trinity. The אַיִל (ayil, "ram") caught בַּסְּבַךְ (basevakh, "in the thicket") by its קַרְנָיו (horns) presents a profound image: Christ crowned with thorns (the thicket) and caught by His divine nature (the horns symbolizing power/kingship). The word תַּחַת (tachat, "instead of/in place of") establishes the theological principle of substitutionary atonement: one life given in exchange for another. This is the first explicit substitutionary sacrifice in Scripture, laying the foundation for the entire Levitical system and its ultimate fulfillment in Christ. St. Irenaeus taught that the ram represented Christ entangled in human nature for our salvation, and that the thicket symbolized the crown of thorns—the entanglement of deity with suffering humanity.

CROSS-REFERENCES:

  → Romans 8:32
    "He who did not spare his own Son (τοῦ ἰδίου υἱοῦ οὐκ ἐφείσατο) but gave him up for us all (ὑπὲρ ἡμῶν πάντων παρέδωκεν αὐτόν), how will he not also with him graciously give us all things?" Paul's οὐκ ἐφείσατο directly translates the LXX of Genesis 22:12: οὐκ ἐφείσω. The striking difference: Abraham was stopped from completing the sacrifice and did spare Isaac ultimately; but the Father completed what Abraham only typified—He did not spare His Son but delivered Him up. The ram was substituted for Isaac; nothing was substituted for Christ. The type points to the greater reality where the Son Himself becomes both Isaac (the willing victim) and the ram (the actual sacrifice).

  → 1 Peter 3:18
    "For Christ also suffered once for sins (ἅπαξ περὶ ἁμαρτιῶν ἔπαθεν), the righteous for the unrighteous (δίκαιος ὑπὲρ ἀδίκων), that he might bring us to God." The word תַּחַת (in place of) becomes ὑπὲρ (on behalf of) in the New Testament vocabulary of substitution. The ram died "in place of" Isaac; Christ died "on behalf of" the unrighteous. The Akedah establishes that divine justice accepts substitutionary satisfaction—a principle without which the Gospel would be incoherent. The "righteous for the unrighteous" echoes the spotless ram (representing the sinless Christ) dying for the guilty son (representing sinful humanity).

  → Isaiah 53:4-5, 10
    "Surely he has borne our griefs (חֳלָיֵנוּ הוּא נָשָׂא) and carried our sorrows... he was pierced for our transgressions (מְחֹלָל מִפְּשָׁעֵנוּ), he was crushed for our iniquities (מְדֻכָּא מֵעֲוֺנֹתֵינוּ)... Yet it was the will of YHWH to crush him (וַיהוָה חָפֵץ דַּכְּאוֹ)." The Suffering Servant passage expands what the Akedah compressed: the substitute not merely dies but bears sins, carries grief, is pierced and crushed. The Hebrew מְחֹלָל (pierced) connects to the ram caught by its horns—held fast, unable to escape, destined for sacrifice. The Father's will (חָפֵץ) to crush the Servant parallels the divine command to Abraham.

  → Hebrews 9:28
    "So Christ, having been offered once to bear the sins of many (ἅπαξ προσενεχθεὶς εἰς τὸ πολλῶν ἀνενεγκεῖν ἁμαρτίας), will appear a second time, not to deal with sin but to save those who are eagerly waiting for him." The word ἅπαξ (once) emphasizes the unrepeatable nature of Christ's sacrifice—unlike the Levitical sacrifices that had to be repeated, the antitype of the ram offered on Moriah is "once for all" (ἐφάπαξ). The ram was offered once; Christ was offered once. But the ram's sacrifice had to be repeated symbolically throughout Israel's history until the true Lamb came.

  → John 19:2, 5
    "And the soldiers twisted together a crown of thorns (στέφανον ἐξ ἀκανθῶν) and put it on his head... Jesus came out, wearing the crown of thorns." The Fathers saw in the ram "caught in the thicket by his horns" a direct prophecy of Christ crowned with thorns. The סְבַךְ (thicket/tangled brush) becomes the ἄκανθαι (thorns) of the Passion. As the ram's horns (symbols of strength and kingship) were entangled in thorns, so Christ's divine majesty was veiled in the shame of thorns—deity caught in humanity's curse (for thorns came as a result of the Fall, Genesis 3:18).

═══════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════
[Genesis 22:14] — YHWH YIREH: "ON THE MOUNTAIN OF YHWH IT SHALL BE PROVIDED"
═══════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════

וַיִּקְרָ֧א אַבְרָהָ֛ם שֵֽׁם־הַמָּק֥וֹם הַה֖וּא יְהוָ֣ה ׀ יִרְאֶ֑ה אֲשֶׁר֙ יֵאָמֵ֣ר הַיּ֔וֹם בְּהַ֥ר יְהוָ֖ה יֵרָאֶֽה׃

"And Abraham called the name of that place 'YHWH Yireh' (יְהוָה יִרְאֶה, 'YHWH will provide/see'), as it is said to this day, 'On the mountain of YHWH it shall be provided/seen' (בְּהַר יְהוָה יֵרָאֶה)."

EXEGESIS:

This verse contains one of the most theologically dense word-plays in Scripture. The name יְהוָה יִרְאֶה (YHWH Yireh) literally means "YHWH will see" but idiomatically means "YHWH will provide"—revealing the Hebrew understanding that God's "seeing" a need and "providing" for it are the same act. The verb ר-א-ה (ra'ah, to see) appears three times in this chapter: Abraham "saw" (וַיַּרְא) the place (v.4), "saw" the ram (v.13), and now names the place using this same root. But the proverb "on the mountain of YHWH יֵרָאֶה (yera'eh)" shifts to the Niphal (passive) form: "it will be seen/provided" or "He will be seen/appear." This creates a stunning prophecy: on this mountain, not only will YHWH provide, but YHWH will be seen—He will appear, manifest, reveal Himself. The Temple would be built on Moriah (2 Chronicles 3:1), where God's glory would dwell visibly. And on a hill nearby (Golgotha), God Himself would be "seen" in the ultimate sense—the Incarnate Son revealed on the cross. St. Cyril of Alexandria taught that Abraham's naming was prophetic of Christ's crucifixion: "On the mountain of YHWH it shall be seen"—and what was seen was God Himself, crucified for humanity.

CROSS-REFERENCES:

  → John 8:56
    "Your father Abraham rejoiced that he would see my day (ἵνα ἴδῃ τὴν ἡμέραν τὴν ἐμήν). He saw it (εἶδεν) and was glad." Jesus explicitly declares that Abraham "saw" (εἶδεν) His day—the Greek ὁράω directly translating the Hebrew ר-א-ה theme of Genesis 22. When did Abraham see Christ's day? On Moriah, when he prophetically named the place "YHWH Will See/Provide" and glimpsed by faith the future sacrifice of God's true יָחִיד (only/unique Son). Abraham's joy came from seeing, beyond Isaac's deliverance, the coming Lamb of God who would make all provision complete.

  → 2 Chronicles 3:1
    "Then Solomon began to build the house of YHWH in Jerusalem on Mount Moriah (בְּהַר הַמּוֹרִיָּה), where YHWH had appeared (נִרְאָה) to David his father, at the place that David had prepared, on the threshing floor of Ornan the Jebusite." The Chronicler explicitly identifies the Temple mount as Moriah—the place where Isaac was bound. The same ר-א-ה root appears: God "appeared" (נִרְאָה, Niphal) there. The progression is clear: on this mountain YHWH provided the ram, YHWH appeared to David, YHWH's glory filled Solomon's Temple, and ultimately YHWH Himself was "seen" on the nearby hill of Calvary in the Person of Christ.

  → Philippians 4:19
    "And my God will supply (πληρώσει) every need of yours according to his riches in glory in Christ Jesus." The promise "YHWH Yireh" echoes through Scripture to Paul's confident declaration: God who provided the ram for Abraham will provide all things for His children. But note the phrase "in Christ Jesus"—all divine provision flows through the Son who was Himself provided on Moriah's mountain. The ram was the immediate provision; Christ is the eternal provision of which the ram was merely a shadow.

  → Hebrews 11:10, 13-16
    "For he was looking forward to the city that has foundations, whose designer and builder is God... These all died in faith, not having received the things promised, but having seen them (ἰδόντες) and greeted them from afar." Abraham's faith "saw" (ἰδόντες) beyond the immediate to the eschatological. When he named Moriah "YHWH will provide," he was not merely grateful for the ram but prophetically declaring that this mountain would be the site of God's ultimate provision. The "city with foundations" is the heavenly Jerusalem, which descends in Revelation 21—the final fulfillment of what Abraham glimpsed through tear-filled eyes on Moriah.

  → Genesis 22:8 (internal reference)
    "Abraham said, 'God will provide Himself the lamb' (אֱלֹהִים יִרְאֶה־לּוֹ הַשֶּׂה)." Verse 8 and verse 14 form an inclusio around the Akedah: both use the verb יִרְאֶה (will provide/see). Abraham's prophecy in v.8 is commemorated in v.14 as the perpetual name of the place. What Abraham spoke in faith ("God will provide") became what Abraham named in gratitude ("YHWH has provided"). And what was named in gratitude became the proverb of hope: "On YHWH's mountain, provision is made"—a proverb that sustained Israel through the Babylonian exile and sustains the Church until Christ returns.

═══════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════
[Genesis 22:16-18] — THE DIVINE OATH: YHWH SWEARS BY HIMSELF TO BLESS ALL NATIONS
═══════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════

בִּ֣י נִשְׁבַּ֗עְתִּי נְאֻם־יְהוָ֓ה כִּ֚י יַ֣עַן אֲשֶׁ֤ר עָשִׂ֙יתָ֙ אֶת־הַדָּבָ֣ר הַזֶּ֔ה וְלֹ֥א חָשַׂ֖כְתָּ אֶת־בִּנְךָ֥ אֶת־יְחִידֶֽךָ׃
כִּֽי־בָרֵ֣ךְ אֲבָרֶכְךָ֗ וְהַרְבָּ֨ה אַרְבֶּ֤ה אֶֽת־זַרְעֲךָ֙ כְּכוֹכְבֵ֣י הַשָּׁמַ֔יִם וְכַח֕וֹל אֲשֶׁ֖ר עַל־שְׂפַ֣ת הַיָּ֑ם וְיִרַ֣שׁ זַרְעֲךָ֔ אֵ֖ת שַׁ֥עַר אֹיְבָֽיו׃
וְהִתְבָּרֲכ֣וּ בְזַרְעֲךָ֔ כֹּ֖ל גּוֹיֵ֣י הָאָ֑רֶץ עֵ֕קֶב אֲשֶׁ֥ר שָׁמַ֖עְתָּ בְּקֹלִֽי׃

"'By Myself I have sworn' (בִּי נִשְׁבַּעְתִּי), declares YHWH (נְאֻם־יְהוָה), 'because you have done this thing (יַעַן אֲשֶׁר עָשִׂיתָ אֶת־הַדָּבָר הַזֶּה) and have not withheld your son, your only son (וְלֹא חָשַׂכְתָּ אֶת־בִּנְךָ אֶת־יְחִידֶךָ): I will surely bless you (כִּי־בָרֵךְ אֲבָרֶכְךָ), and I will surely multiply your seed (וְהַרְבָּה אַרְבֶּה אֶת־זַרְעֲךָ) as the stars of the heavens (כְּכוֹכְבֵי הַשָּׁמַיִם) and as the sand on the seashore (וְכַחוֹל אֲשֶׁר עַל־שְׂפַת הַיָּם). And your seed shall possess the gate of his enemies (וְיִרַשׁ זַרְעֲךָ אֵת שַׁעַר אֹיְבָיו). And in your seed all the nations of the earth shall be blessed (וְהִתְבָּרֲכוּ בְזַרְעֲךָ כֹּל גּוֹיֵי הָאָרֶץ), because you have obeyed My voice (עֵקֶב אֲשֶׁר שָׁמַעְתָּ בְּקֹלִי).'"

EXEGESIS:

This is the only place in Scripture where YHWH swears an oath by Himself (בִּי נִשְׁבַּעְתִּי)—an act of such theological magnitude that Hebrews 6:13-17 explicates it at length. Because there is none greater than God, He swears by His own being, making the promise doubly immutable: the word of the One who cannot lie, sealed by an oath that cannot be broken. The Infinitive Absolute constructions (בָרֵךְ אֲבָרֶכְךָ, "blessing I will bless"; הַרְבָּה אַרְבֶּה, "multiplying I will multiply") express emphatic certainty—grammatical superlatives reinforcing the oath. The word זֶרַע (zera', "seed/offspring") is grammatically singular, and Paul seizes upon this in Galatians 3:16 to identify Christ as the ultimate "Seed" through whom all nations are blessed. The phrase "your seed shall possess the gate of his enemies" (וְיִרַשׁ זַרְעֲךָ אֵת שַׁעַר אֹיְבָיו) uses the singular verb יִרַשׁ, further confirming the singular Seed—Christ who conquered death and Hades, possessing the "gates of hell" (Matthew 16:18). The universal scope—"all the nations of the earth" (כֹּל גּוֹיֵי הָאָרֶץ)—marks this as the gospel preached beforehand to Abraham (Galatians 3:8). St. John Chrysostom taught that the Akedah was the pivotal moment when God's promise to Abraham reached its fullest expression: the willingness to offer Isaac unlocked the oath that would bless all humanity through Christ.

CROSS-REFERENCES:

  → Hebrews 6:13-17
    "For when God made a promise to Abraham, since he had no one greater by whom to swear, he swore by himself (ὤμοσεν καθ' ἑαυτοῦ), saying, 'Surely I will bless you and multiply you'... So when God desired to show more convincingly to the heirs of the promise the unchangeable character of his purpose (τὸ ἀμετάθετον τῆς βουλῆς αὐτοῦ), he guaranteed it with an oath (ἐμεσίτευσεν ὅρκῳ)." The author of Hebrews identifies this oath as the foundation of Christian assurance. God's two "unchangeable things" (ἀμετάθετα)—His word and His oath—provide an "anchor for the soul" (ἄγκυρα τῆς ψυχῆς). What was promised to Abraham flows through the oath to all who share Abraham's faith.

  → Galatians 3:8, 16
    "And the Scripture, foreseeing that God would justify the Gentiles by faith, preached the gospel beforehand to Abraham (προευηγγελίσατο τῷ Ἀβραάμ), saying, 'In you shall all the nations be blessed'... Now the promises were made to Abraham and to his offspring (τῷ σπέρματι αὐτοῦ). It does not say, 'And to offsprings' (τοῖς σπέρμασιν), referring to many, but referring to one, 'And to your offspring' (τῷ σπέρματί σου), who is Christ." Paul's grammatical argument identifies the singular זֶרַע as Christ Himself. The Akedah oath is thus the "gospel preached beforehand"—good news that through Abraham's Seed (Christ), all nations would receive the blessing of justification by faith.

  → Acts 3:25-26
    "You are the sons of the prophets and of the covenant that God made with your fathers, saying to Abraham, 'And in your offspring shall all the families of the earth be blessed' (ἐνευλογηθήσονται πᾶσαι αἱ πατριαὶ τῆς γῆς). God, having raised up his servant (ἀναστήσας τὸν παῖδα αὐτοῦ), sent him to you first, to bless you." Peter identifies Jesus as the fulfillment of the Abrahamic oath. The word παῖς (servant/son) connects to Isaiah's Servant Songs and to Isaac as Abraham's "son." The blessing promised through Abraham's seed is now dispensed through the risen Christ—"raised up" (ἀναστήσας) echoing both resurrection and prophetic commissioning.

  → Luke 1:54-55, 72-73
    "He has helped his servant Israel, in remembrance of his mercy, as he spoke to our fathers, to Abraham and to his offspring forever... to show the mercy promised to our fathers and to remember his holy covenant, the oath that he swore to our father Abraham (ὅρκον ὃν ὤμοσεν πρὸς Ἀβραὰμ τὸν πατέρα ἡμῶν)." Mary's Magnificat and Zechariah's Benedictus both celebrate the fulfillment of the Abrahamic oath in Christ's coming. The incarnation is presented as God "remembering" (μνησθῆναι) His oath—not that God had forgotten, but that the moment of fulfillment had arrived. The oath sworn on Moriah reaches its terminus in Bethlehem.

  → Matthew 16:18
    "And I tell you, you are Peter, and on this rock I will build my church, and the gates of hell (πύλαι ᾅδου) shall not prevail against it." Christ's declaration echoes the Abrahamic promise that His seed would "possess the gate of his enemies" (שַׁעַר אֹיְבָיו). The singular "seed" (Christ) builds His Church which possesses the gates of Hades itself—death and Satan are conquered. What was promised to Abraham on Moriah is fulfilled at Caesarea Philippi and consummated at Golgotha and the empty tomb.

────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────


═══════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════
              פרק כג — CHAPTER 23: THE DEATH OF SARAH AND THE CAVE OF MACHPELAH
                     שְׂדֵה הַמַּכְפֵּלָה — THE FIELD OF THE DOUBLE CAVE
═══════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
[Genesis 23:1-4] — SARAH'S DEATH AND ABRAHAM AS STRANGER AND SOJOURNER
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────

וַיִּהְיוּ֙ חַיֵּ֣י שָׂרָ֔ה מֵאָ֥ה שָׁנָ֛ה וְעֶשְׂרִ֥ים שָׁנָ֖ה וְשֶׁ֣בַע שָׁנִ֑ים שְׁנֵ֖י חַיֵּ֥י שָׂרָֽה׃
וַתָּ֣מָת שָׂרָ֗ה בְּקִרְיַ֥ת אַרְבַּ֛ע הִ֥וא חֶבְר֖וֹן בְּאֶ֣רֶץ כְּנָ֑עַן וַיָּבֹא֙ אַבְרָהָ֔ם לִסְפֹּ֥ד לְשָׂרָ֖ה וְלִבְכֹּתָֽהּ׃
וַיָּ֙קָם֙ אַבְרָהָ֔ם מֵעַ֖ל פְּנֵ֣י מֵת֑וֹ וַיְדַבֵּ֥ר אֶל־בְּנֵי־חֵ֖ת לֵאמֹֽר׃ גֵּר־וְתוֹשָׁ֥ב אָנֹכִ֖י עִמָּכֶ֑ם תְּנ֨וּ לִ֤י אֲחֻזַּת־קֶ֙בֶר֙ עִמָּכֶ֔ם וְאֶקְבְּרָ֥ה מֵתִ֖י מִלְּפָנָֽי׃

"And the life of Sarah was one hundred years and twenty years and seven years (מֵאָה שָׁנָה וְעֶשְׂרִים שָׁנָה וְשֶׁבַע שָׁנִים)—the years of the life of Sarah (שְׁנֵי חַיֵּי שָׂרָה). And Sarah died in Kiriath-arba (בְּקִרְיַת אַרְבַּע)—that is, Hebron (הִוא חֶבְרוֹן)—in the land of Canaan (בְּאֶרֶץ כְּנָעַן). And Abraham came to mourn for Sarah and to weep for her (לִסְפֹּד לְשָׂרָה וְלִבְכֹּתָהּ). And Abraham rose up from before his dead (וַיָּקָם אַבְרָהָם מֵעַל פְּנֵי מֵתוֹ) and spoke to the sons of Heth, saying: 'I am a stranger and a sojourner among you (גֵּר־וְתוֹשָׁב אָנֹכִי עִמָּכֶם). Give me a possession of a burial place among you (תְּנוּ לִי אֲחֻזַּת־קֶבֶר עִמָּכֶם), that I may bury my dead out of my sight (וְאֶקְבְּרָה מֵתִי מִלְּפָנָי).'"

EXEGESIS:

Sarah is the only woman in Scripture whose age at death is recorded (127 years), indicating her unique covenantal significance as the mother of the promised seed. The Hebrew unusually separates her age into three parts—"one hundred years and twenty years and seven years"—which the rabbis interpreted as indicating that at 100 she was like 20 in beauty, and at 20 she was like 7 in innocence. The phrase גֵּר־וְתוֹשָׁב (ger-vetoshav, "stranger and sojourner") is a legal terminus technicus: a גֵּר (ger) is an alien without land rights; a תּוֹשָׁב (toshav) is a resident alien with temporary dwelling rights. Abraham confesses himself both—permanently landless in the land of promise. This dual status becomes paradigmatic for all believers: citizens of heaven, strangers on earth. The verbs לִסְפֹּד (lispod, "to mourn/eulogize") and לִבְכֹּת (livkot, "to weep") distinguish public ritual mourning from private grief. Abraham's only earthly possession would be a grave—a profound statement that his true inheritance lay beyond this world. St. Gregory of Nyssa taught that Abraham's purchase of Machpelah demonstrated that the patriarchs possessed the land only in hope, holding it as a pledge of the heavenly country.

CROSS-REFERENCES:

  → Hebrews 11:9-10, 13-16
    "By faith he went to live in the land of promise, as in a foreign land (ὡς ἀλλοτρίαν), living in tents with Isaac and Jacob, heirs with him of the same promise. For he was looking forward to the city that has foundations (τὴν τοὺς θεμελίους ἔχουσαν πόλιν), whose designer and builder is God... These all died in faith, not having received the things promised, but having seen them and greeted them from afar, and having acknowledged that they were strangers and exiles on the earth (ξένοι καὶ παρεπίδημοι)." The Hebrews passage directly interprets Abraham's confession "stranger and sojourner" (גֵּר־וְתוֹשָׁב = ξένοι καὶ παρεπίδημοι). Abraham's only land purchase was a tomb—he possessed death while awaiting the city of life. The "city with foundations" contrasts with his tent-dwelling; the heavenly architect contrasts with the Canaanite landowners.

  → 1 Peter 2:11
    "Beloved, I urge you as sojourners and exiles (παροίκους καὶ παρεπιδήμους) to abstain from the passions of the flesh, which wage war against your soul." Peter applies Abraham's self-description to all Christians. The παροίκους (resident aliens) corresponds to תּוֹשָׁב; the παρεπιδήμους (temporary residents) corresponds to גֵּר. Christians live in the world but belong to another kingdom—purchasing graves here while awaiting resurrection elsewhere.

  → Acts 7:5
    "Yet he gave him no inheritance in it (οὐκ ἔδωκεν αὐτῷ κληρονομίαν ἐν αὐτῇ), not even a foot's length (οὐδὲ βῆμα ποδός), but promised to give it to him as a possession and to his offspring after him." Stephen's speech highlights the paradox: God promised the land, but Abraham owned nothing except a burial cave. The "foot's length" (βῆμα ποδός) emphasizes the total absence of inheritance. Yet this landlessness was not divine failure but pedagogical preparation—teaching that the true inheritance transcends earthly territory.

  → Psalm 39:12 (LXX 38:13)
    "Hear my prayer, O YHWH, and give ear to my cry; hold not your peace at my tears! For I am a sojourner with you (כִּי גֵר אָנֹכִי עִמָּךְ), a guest (תּוֹשָׁב), like all my fathers." David adopts Abraham's language, confessing himself a "stranger and sojourner" before God. The phrase עִמָּךְ (with You) transforms the meaning: not merely landless on earth but a guest in God's household, awaiting the permanent dwelling that earthly Canaan could never provide.

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
[Genesis 23:17-20] — THE PURCHASE OF MACHPELAH: ABRAHAM'S ONLY EARTHLY INHERITANCE
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────

וַיָּ֣קָם ׀ שְׂדֵ֣ה עֶפְר֗וֹן אֲשֶׁר֙ בַּמַּכְפֵּלָ֔ה אֲשֶׁ֖ר לִפְנֵ֣י מַמְרֵ֑א הַשָּׂדֶה֙ וְהַמְּעָרָ֣ה אֲשֶׁר־בּ֔וֹ וְכָל־הָעֵץ֙ אֲשֶׁ֣ר בַּשָּׂדֶ֔ה אֲשֶׁ֥ר בְּכָל־גְּבֻל֖וֹ סָבִֽיב׃
לְאַבְרָהָ֥ם לְמִקְנָ֖ה לְעֵינֵ֣י בְנֵי־חֵ֑ת בְּכֹ֖ל בָּאֵ֥י שַֽׁעַר־עִירֽוֹ׃ וְאַחֲרֵי־כֵ֞ן קָבַ֣ר אַבְרָהָ֗ם אֶת־שָׂרָה֙ אִשְׁתּ֔וֹ אֶל־מְעָרַ֛ת שְׂדֵ֥ה הַמַּכְפֵּלָ֖ה עַל־פְּנֵ֣י מַמְרֵ֑א הִ֥וא חֶבְר֖וֹן בְּאֶ֥רֶץ כְּנָֽעַן׃
וַיָּ֨קָם הַשָּׂדֶ֜ה וְהַמְּעָרָ֧ה אֲשֶׁר־בּ֛וֹ לְאַבְרָהָ֖ם לַאֲחֻזַּת־קָ֑בֶר מֵאֵ֖ת בְּנֵי־חֵֽת׃

"So the field of Ephron in Machpelah (שְׂדֵה עֶפְרוֹן אֲשֶׁר בַּמַּכְפֵּלָה), which was before Mamre (לִפְנֵי מַמְרֵא)—the field and the cave that was in it, and all the trees in the field, throughout its entire border (בְּכָל־גְּבֻלוֹ סָבִיב)—was made over to Abraham as a possession (לְאַבְרָהָם לְמִקְנָה) in the presence of the sons of Heth, before all who went in at the gate of his city (בְּכֹל בָּאֵי שַׁעַר־עִירוֹ). After this, Abraham buried Sarah his wife in the cave of the field of Machpelah facing Mamre (אֶל־מְעָרַת שְׂדֵה הַמַּכְפֵּלָה עַל־פְּנֵי מַמְרֵא)—that is, Hebron—in the land of Canaan. And the field and the cave in it were made over to Abraham for a possession of a burial place (לַאֲחֻזַּת־קָבֶר) from the sons of Heth."

EXEGESIS:

The name מַכְפֵּלָה (Machpelah) derives from כָּפַל (kafal, "to double/fold"), likely referring to a double cave or a cave with two chambers. This became the patriarchal sepulcher where Abraham, Sarah, Isaac, Rebekah, Jacob, and Leah would all be buried—making it one of the holiest sites in biblical history. The legal terminology in this passage—the detailed enumeration of field, cave, trees, and boundaries "in the presence of witnesses" at the city gate—reflects ancient Near Eastern property transfer contracts. The phrase וַיָּקָם (vayyaqam, literally "it arose/stood") is a legal term meaning "it was confirmed/established"—the property "stood" as Abraham's undisputed possession. Yet this single plot of land, purchased for 400 shekels of silver, was the patriarch's only earthly inheritance in the land promised to his descendants. St. Ambrose saw in this purchase a profound spiritual lesson: Abraham bought death (a grave) with earthly currency, but received life (the promise) by divine grace. The cave awaiting resurrection bodies becomes a symbol of faith in the God who raises the dead.

CROSS-REFERENCES:

  → Acts 7:5, 16
    "Yet he gave him no inheritance in it (οὐκ ἔδωκεν αὐτῷ κληρονομίαν), not even a foot's length, but promised to give it to him as a possession... And [Jacob's sons] were carried back to Shechem and laid in the tomb that Abraham had bought (ἐν τῷ μνήματι ᾧ ὠνήσατο Ἀβραὰμ) for a sum of silver." Stephen emphasizes the paradox: the land of promise yielded only a burial cave for Abraham's purchase. The elaborate legal transaction for Machpelah contrasts with God's promise of the entire land—Abraham paid for death while waiting for life. Note: Stephen mentions Shechem (possibly conflating with Jacob's purchase in Genesis 33:19), but the Machpelah tomb at Hebron remained the primary patriarchal burial site.

  → Hebrews 11:16
    "But as it is, they desire a better country, that is, a heavenly one (κρείττονος ὀρέγονται, τοῦτ᾽ ἔστιν ἐπουρανίου). Therefore God is not ashamed to be called their God, for he has prepared for them a city (ἡτοίμασεν γὰρ αὐτοῖς πόλιν)." The patriarchs' contentment with a single burial cave demonstrated their longing for the "better country." God's willingness to be called "the God of Abraham, Isaac, and Jacob" (Matthew 22:32) is tied to their pilgrim faith. The cave at Machpelah was not their ultimate destination but a waystation—the true city awaited them beyond death.

  → Genesis 49:29-32
    "Then he charged them and said to them, 'I am to be gathered to my people; bury me with my fathers in the cave that is in the field of Ephron the Hittite, in the cave that is in the field of Machpelah (בִּמְעָרָה אֲשֶׁר בִּשְׂדֵה הַמַּכְפֵּלָה)... There they buried Abraham and Sarah his wife. There they buried Isaac and Rebekah his wife. And there I buried Leah.'" Jacob's dying request confirms Machpelah as the sacred patriarchal cemetery. Six of the patriarchs and matriarchs rest there, awaiting resurrection. The cave purchased for Sarah became the gathering place of the covenant family—a tangible claim on the promised land held in trust for the resurrection.

  → Genesis 50:13
    "For his sons carried him to the land of Canaan and buried him in the cave of the field at Machpelah (וַיִּקְבְּרוּ אֹתוֹ בִּמְעָרַת שְׂדֵה הַמַּכְפֵּלָה)... which Abraham bought with the field from Ephron the Hittite to possess as a burial site." Jacob's burial completes the patriarchal interment at Machpelah. The phrase "to possess" (לַאֲחֻזָּה) echoes the original purchase—the only possession in the land was a cemetery. Yet this possession was prophetic: the patriarchs possessed in death what they would inherit in resurrection, when the God of the living (not the dead) fulfills every promise.

────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────


═══════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════
              פרק כד — CHAPTER 24: A BRIDE FOR ISAAC (REBEKAH)
               הַשָּׁלִיחַ וְהַכַּלָּה — THE SERVANT AND THE BRIDE
═══════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
[Genesis 24:1-4] — THE COMMISSION: A BRIDE FROM THE FATHER'S HOUSEHOLD
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────

וְאַבְרָהָ֣ם זָקֵ֔ן בָּ֖א בַּיָּמִ֑ים וַֽיהוָ֛ה בֵּרַ֥ךְ אֶת־אַבְרָהָ֖ם בַּכֹּֽל׃
וַיֹּ֣אמֶר אַבְרָהָ֗ם אֶל־עַבְדּוֹ֙ זְקַ֣ן בֵּית֔וֹ הַמֹּשֵׁ֖ל בְּכָל־אֲשֶׁר־ל֑וֹ שִׂים־נָ֥א יָדְךָ֖ תַּ֥חַת יְרֵכִֽי׃
וְאַשְׁבִּ֣יעֲךָ֔ בַּֽיהוָה֙ אֱלֹהֵ֣י הַשָּׁמַ֔יִם וֵֽאלֹהֵ֖י הָאָ֑רֶץ אֲשֶׁ֨ר לֹֽא־תִקַּ֤ח אִשָּׁה֙ לִבְנִ֔י מִבְּנוֹת֙ הַֽכְּנַעֲנִ֔י אֲשֶׁ֥ר אָנֹכִ֖י יוֹשֵׁ֥ב בְּקִרְבּֽוֹ׃
כִּ֧י אֶל־אַרְצִ֛י וְאֶל־מוֹלַדְתִּ֖י תֵּלֵ֑ךְ וְלָקַחְתָּ֥ אִשָּׁ֖ה לִבְנִ֥י לְיִצְחָֽק׃

"And Abraham was old, advanced in days (זָקֵן בָּא בַּיָּמִים), and YHWH had blessed Abraham in all things (וַיהוָה בֵּרַךְ אֶת־אַבְרָהָם בַּכֹּל). And Abraham said to his servant, the elder of his house (עַבְדּוֹ זְקַן בֵּיתוֹ), who ruled over all that he had (הַמֹּשֵׁל בְּכָל־אֲשֶׁר־לוֹ): 'Put your hand under my thigh (שִׂים־נָא יָדְךָ תַּחַת יְרֵכִי), and I will make you swear by YHWH, the God of heaven and the God of earth (בַּיהוָה אֱלֹהֵי הַשָּׁמַיִם וֵאלֹהֵי הָאָרֶץ), that you shall not take a wife for my son from the daughters of the Canaanites among whom I dwell (לֹא־תִקַּח אִשָּׁה לִבְנִי מִבְּנוֹת הַכְּנַעֲנִי). But you shall go to my country and to my kindred (אֶל־אַרְצִי וְאֶל־מוֹלַדְתִּי), and take a wife for my son Isaac (וְלָקַחְתָּ אִשָּׁה לִבְנִי לְיִצְחָק).'"

EXEGESIS:

This chapter—the longest in Genesis—presents one of Scripture's richest typological tableaux: the Father (Abraham) sends a servant to procure a bride for his son (Isaac). The Fathers universally identified this as prefiguring the Father sending the Holy Spirit to call forth the Church as the Bride of Christ.
